<p><b>Moscow</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/oʊ/ long 'o' in 'code'">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>, <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/aʊ/ 'ou' in 'pout'">aʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_English" title="Help:IPA for English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˈ/ primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="'m' in 'my'">m</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'s' in 'sigh'">s</span><span title="'k' in 'kind'">k</span><span title="/ɒ/ short 'o' in 'body'">ɒ</span><span title="'v' in 'vie'">v</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">Москва́</span>, <small><a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Russian" title="Romanization of Russian">tr.</a></small> <i>Moskva</i>; <small>IPA: </small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA_for_Russian" title="Help:IPA for Russian">[mɐˈskva]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<a href="/wiki/File:Ru-Moskva.ogg" title="File:Ru-Moskva.ogg"><img alt="" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="500" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/13px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/20px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Speaker_Icon.svg/26px-Speaker_Icon.svg.png 2x" width="13"/></a> <a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/dd/Ru-Moskva.ogg" title="Ru-Moskva.ogg">listen</a>)</small>) is the <a href="/wiki/Capital_city" title="Capital city">capital</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_and_towns_in_Russia_by_population" title="List of cities and towns in Russia by population">most populous city</a> of <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, with 12.2 million residents within the <a href="/wiki/City_limits" title="City limits">city limits</a> and 16.8 million within the <a href="/wiki/Urban_area" title="Urban area">urban area</a>. Moscow has the status of a <a href="/wiki/Federal_cities_of_Russia" title="Federal cities of Russia">Russian federal city</a>.</p>
<p>Moscow is a major political, economic, cultural, and scientific center of Russia and <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Largest_cities_in_Europe" title="Largest cities in Europe">largest city</a> entirely on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_continent" title="European continent">European continent</a>. By broader definitions Moscow is among the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/World%27s_largest_cities" title="World's largest cities">world's largest cities</a>, being the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_by_population" title="List of metropolitan areas by population">14th largest metro area</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_agglomerations_by_population" title="List of agglomerations by population">18th largest agglomeration</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_urban_areas_by_population" title="List of urban areas by population">15th largest urban area</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_proper_by_population" title="List of cities proper by population">11th largest by population within city limits</a> worldwide. According to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Forbes_list_of_billionaires" title="Forbes list of billionaires">Forbes 2013</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> Moscow has been ranked as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_expensive_cities_for_expatriate_employees" title="List of most expensive cities for expatriate employees">ninth most expensive city in the world by Mercer</a> and has one of the world's <a href="/wiki/List_of_cities_by_GDP" title="List of cities by GDP">largest urban economies</a>, being ranked as an <a href="/wiki/Global_city" title="Global city">alpha global city</a> according to the <a href="/wiki/Globalization_and_World_Cities_Research_Network" title="Globalization and World Cities Research Network">Globalization and World Cities Research Network</a>, and is also one of the fastest growing tourist destinations in the world according to the <a href="/wiki/Tourism#World_tourism_statistics_and_rankings" title="Tourism">MasterCard Global Destination Cities Index</a>. Moscow is the northernmost and coldest <a href="/wiki/Megacity" title="Megacity">megacity</a> and <a href="/wiki/Metropolis" title="Metropolis">metropolis</a> on <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>. It is home to the <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">tallest free standing structure in Europe</a>; the <a href="/wiki/Federation_Tower" title="Federation Tower">Federation Tower</a>, the tallest skyscraper in Europe; and the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_International_Business_Center" title="Moscow International Business Center">Moscow International Business Center</a>. By its territorial expansion on July 1, 2012 southwest into the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Oblast" title="Moscow Oblast">Moscow Oblast</a>, the area of the capital more than doubled, going from 1,091 to 2,511 square kilometers (421 to 970 sq mi), and it gained an additional population of 233,000 people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Merger2_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Merger2-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moscow is situated on the <a href="/wiki/Moskva_River" title="Moskva River">Moskva River</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central Federal District</a> of <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, making it the world's most populated inland city. The city is well known for its architecture, particularly its historic buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil's Cathedral">Saint Basil's Cathedral</a> with its brightly colored domes. With over 40 percent of its territory covered by greenery, it is one of the greenest capitals and major cities in Europe and the world, having the largest forest in an urban area within its borders—more than any other major city—even before its expansion in 2012.</p>
<p>The city has served as the capital of a progression of states, from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a> and the subsequent <a href="/wiki/Tsardom_of_Russia" title="Tsardom of Russia">Tsardom of Russia</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> and the contemporary <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a>. Moscow is considered the center of Russian culture, having served as the home of Russian artists, scientists and sports figures and because of the presence of museums, academic and political institutions and theaters.</p>
<p>Moscow is the seat of power of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_government" title="Russian government">Government of Russia</a>, being the site of the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a>, a medieval city-fortress that is today the residence for work of the <a href="/wiki/President_of_Russia" title="President of Russia">President of Russia</a>. The Moscow Kremlin and <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a> are also one of several <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Sites</a> in the city. Both chambers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_parliament" title="Russian parliament">Russian parliament</a> (the <a href="/wiki/State_Duma" title="State Duma">State Duma</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Federation_Council_of_Russia" title="Federation Council of Russia">Federation Council</a>) also sit in the city.</p>
<p>The city is served by a transit network, which includes four international <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Airports" title="Airports">airports</a>, nine railway terminals, numerous trams, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_monorail" title="Moscow monorail">a monorail system</a> and one of the deepest underground rapid transit systems in the world, the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Metro" title="Moscow Metro">Moscow Metro</a>, the fourth-largest in the world and largest outside of <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> in terms of passenger numbers, and the busiest in Europe. It is recognized as one of the city's landmarks due to the rich architecture of its 200 stations.</p>
<p>Moscow has acquired a number of <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a>, most referring to its size and preeminent status within the nation: The <a href="/wiki/Third_Rome" title="Third Rome">Third Rome</a> (<span lang="ru">Третий Рим</span>), The Whitestone One (<span lang="ru">Белокаменная</span>), The First Throne (<span lang="ru">Первопрестольная</span>), The Forty Forties (<span lang="ru">Сорок Сороков</span>), and The Hero City (<span lang="ru">город-герой</span>). In old Russian the word "<span lang="ru">Сорок</span>" (<i>forty</i>) also meant a church administrative district, which consisted of about forty churches. The <a href="/wiki/Demonym" title="Demonym">demonym</a> for a Moscow resident is "<span lang="ru">москвич</span>" (<i>moskvich</i>) for male or "<span lang="ru">москвич</span>ка" (<i>moskvichka</i>) for female, rendered in English as <i>Muscovite</i>.</p>
<p></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology">Etymology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Etymology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The name of the city is thought to be derived from the name of the <a href="/wiki/Moskva_River" title="Moskva River">Moskva River</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup> There have been proposed several theories of the origin of the name of the river. The most linguistically well-grounded and widely accepted is from the Proto-Balto-Slavic root *<i>mŭzg</i>-/<i>muzg</i>- from the Proto-Indo-European *<i>meu</i>- "wet",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trubach_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trubach-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> so the name <i>Moskva</i> might signify a river at a wetland or a marsh.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognates</a> include <a href="/wiki/Russian_language" title="Russian language">Russian</a>: <span lang="ru">музга</span>, <i>muzga</i> "pool, puddle", <a href="/wiki/Lithuanian_language" title="Lithuanian language">Lithuanian</a>: <span lang="lt"><i>mazgoti</i></span> and <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>: <span lang="lv"><i>mazgāt</i></span> "to wash", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sanskrit_language" title="Sanskrit language">Sanskrit</a>: <span lang="sa">majjati</span> "to drown", <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <span lang="la"><i>mergō</i></span> "to dip, immerse".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-2"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trubach_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Trubach-18">[18]</a></sup> There exist as well similar place names in Poland like <a href="/wiki/Mozgawa" title="Mozgawa">Mozgawa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-3"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-2"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trubach_18-2"><a href="#cite_note-Trubach-18">[18]</a></sup></p>
<p>The original Old Russian form of the name is reconstructed as *<span lang="ru">Москы</span>, *<i>Mosky</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-4"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-3"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup> hence it was one of a few Slavic <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:Proto-Slavic_v-stem_nouns" title="wikt:Category:Proto-Slavic v-stem nouns"><i>ū</i>-stem nouns</a>. As with other nouns of that declension, it had been undergoing a morphological transformation at the early stage of the development of the language, as a result the first written mentions in the 12th century were <span lang="ru">Московь</span>, <i>Moskovĭ</i> (<a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">accusative case</a>), <span lang="ru">Москви</span>, <i>Moskvi</i> (<a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">locative case</a>), <span lang="ru">Москвe/Москвѣ</span>, <i>Moskve/Moskvě</i> (<a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-5"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-4"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup> From the latter forms came the modern Russian name <span lang="ru">Москва</span>, <i>Moskva</i>, which is a result of <a href="/wiki/Morphological_leveling" title="Morphological leveling">morphological generalization</a> with the numerous Slavic <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/Category:Proto-Slavic_hard_a-stem_nouns" title="wikt:Category:Proto-Slavic hard a-stem nouns"><i>ā</i>-stem nouns</a>.</p>
<p>However, the form <i>Moskovĭ</i> has left some traces in many other languages, such as English: Moscow, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <span lang="de"><i>Moskau</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>: <span lang="fr"><i>Moscou</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Latvian_language" title="Latvian language">Latvian</a>: <span lang="lv"><i>Maskava</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ota-Arab">Moskov</span>‎, <a href="/wiki/Tatar_language" title="Tatar language">Tatar</a>: <span lang="tt">Мәскәү, <i>Mäskäw</i></span>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>: <span lang="kk">Мәскеу, <i>Mäskew</i></span>, <span class="Unicode"><a href="/wiki/Chuvash_language" title="Chuvash language">Chuvash</a>: <span lang="cv">Мускав, <i>Muskav</i></span></span>, etc. In a similar manner the Latin name <i>Moscovia</i> has been formed, later it became a colloquial name for Russia used in Western Europe in the 16th–17th centuries. From it as well came English <i>Muscovy</i>.</p>
<p>Various other theories (of Celtic, Iranian, Caucasic, Finno-Ugrian origin), having little or no scientific ground, are now largely rejected by contemporary linguists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vasmer_16-6"><a href="#cite_note-Vasmer-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Smol_17-5"><a href="#cite_note-Smol-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<p>There has been as well a naïve scholastic etymology that connected the name of <a href="/wiki/Meshech" title="Meshech">Mosoch</a>, a son of <a href="/wiki/Japheth" title="Japheth">Japheth</a>, with the name of the city, so that it was thought that the biblical figure was a forefather of Russians as well as other Slavs. The surface similarity of the name <i>Russia</i> with <i>Rosh</i>, an obscure biblical tribe or country, which is mentioned alongside with Mosoch in Ezekiel (38:2–3, 39:1), strengthened up such etymologies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Moscow" title="History of Moscow">History of Moscow</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Moscow_history" title="Timeline of Moscow history">Timeline of Moscow history</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prehistory">Prehistory</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Prehistory">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The oldest evidence of humans on the territory of Moscow dates from the <a href="/wiki/Neolithic" title="Neolithic">Neolithic</a> (Schukinskaya site on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_River" title="Moscow River">Moscow River</a>). Within the modern bounds of the city other late evidence was discovered (the burial ground of the <a href="/wiki/Fatyanovo%E2%80%93Balanovo_culture" title="Fatyanovo–Balanovo culture">Fatyanovskaya culture</a>, the site of the <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> settlement of the <a href="/wiki/Dyakovo_culture" title="Dyakovo culture">Dyakovo culture</a>), on the territory of the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Kremlin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sparrow_Hills" title="Sparrow Hills">Sparrow Hills</a>, <a href="/wiki/Setun_River" title="Setun River">Setun River</a> and Kuntsevskiy forest park, etc.</p>
<p>In the 9th century, the <a href="/wiki/Oka_River" title="Oka River">Oka River</a> was part of the <a href="/wiki/Volga_trade_route" title="Volga trade route">Volga trade route</a>, and the upper Volga watershed became an area of contact between the indigenous Uralic peoples such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merya" title="Merya">Merya</a> and the expanding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgars" title="Volga Bulgars">Volga Bulgars</a> (particularly the second son of Khan Kubrat who expanded the borders of the <a href="/wiki/Old_Great_Bulgaria" title="Old Great Bulgaria">Old Great Bulgaria</a>), <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic</a> (<a href="/wiki/Varangians" title="Varangians">Varangians</a>) and <a href="/wiki/Early_Slavs" title="Early Slavs">Slavic</a> peoples.</p>
<p>The earliest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_East_Slavs" title="Early East Slavs">East Slavic</a> tribes recorded as having expanded to the upper Volga in the 9th to 10th centuries are the <a href="/wiki/Vyatichi" title="Vyatichi">Vyatichi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Krivichi" title="Krivichi">Krivichi</a>. The Moskva River was incorporated as part of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rostov-Suzdal" title="Rostov-Suzdal">Rostov-Suzdal</a> into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus" title="Kievan Rus">Kievan Rus</a> in the 11th century. By AD 1100, a minor settlement had appeared on the mouth of the <a href="/wiki/Neglinnaya_River" title="Neglinnaya River">Neglinnaya River</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_history_.281147.E2.80.931283.29">Early history (1147–1283)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Early history (1147–1283)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus" title="Kievan Rus">Kievan Rus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vladimir-Suzdal" title="Vladimir-Suzdal">Vladimir-Suzdal</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:162px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2856" data-file-width="2301" height="199" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png/160px-Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png/240px-Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png/320px-Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png 2x" width="160"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kyivan_Rus%27_1220-1240.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The principality on the northeastern periphery of <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir-Suzdal" title="Vladimir-Suzdal">Vladimir-Suzdal</a>, grew into the Grand Duchy of Moscow</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The first known reference to Moscow dates from 1147 as a meeting place of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yuri_Dolgoruky" title="Yuri Dolgoruky">Yuri Dolgoruky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sviatoslav_Olgovich" title="Sviatoslav Olgovich">Sviatoslav Olgovich</a>. At the time it was a minor town on the western border of Vladimir-Suzdal Principality.</p>
<p>In 1156, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Knjaz" title="Knjaz">Knjaz</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yury_Dolgoruky" title="Yury Dolgoruky">Yury Dolgoruky</a> fortified the town with a timber fence and a moat. In the course of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Rus" title="Mongol invasion of Rus">Mongol invasion of Rus</a>, the Mongols under <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu Khan</a> burned the city to the ground and killed its inhabitants.</p>
<p>The timber fort <i>na Moskvě</i> "on the Moscow river" was inherited by <a href="/wiki/Daniel_of_Moscow" title="Daniel of Moscow">Daniel</a>, the youngest son of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Nevsky" title="Alexander Nevsky">Alexander Nevsky</a>, in the 1260s, at the time considered the least valuable of his father's possessions. Daniel was still a child at the time, and the big fort was governed by <i>tiuns</i> (deputies), appointed by Daniel's paternal uncle, <a href="/wiki/Yaroslav_of_Tver" title="Yaroslav of Tver">Yaroslav of Tver</a>.</p>
<p>Daniel came of age in the 1270s and became involved in the power struggles of the principality with lasting success, siding with his brother <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_of_Pereslavl" title="Dmitry of Pereslavl">Dmitry</a> in his bid for the rule of Novgorod. From 1283 he acted as the ruler of an independent principality alongside Dmitry, who became Grand Duke of Vladimir. Daniel has been credited with founding the first Moscow monasteries, dedicated to the Lord's Epiphany and to Saint Daniel.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Grand_Duchy_.281283.E2.80.931547.29">Grand Duchy (1283–1547)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Grand Duchy (1283–1547)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Moscow" title="Grand Duchy of Moscow">Grand Duchy of Moscow</a></div>
<table class="graytable" style="text-align:center">
<tr>

</tr>

</tr>
</table>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg/220px-Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg/330px-Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg/440px-Andronikov_Monastery_18.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

Spassky Cathedral, 1357 (the oldest extant building in Moscow)</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3747" data-file-width="2501" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg/220px-0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg/330px-0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg/440px-0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:0_4707b_6549aa91_spasskaya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Spasskaya_Tower" title="Spasskaya Tower">Spasskaya Tower</a> was built in 1491</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Daniel I ruled Moscow as Grand Duke until 1303 and established it as a prosperous city which would eclipse its parent principality of Vladimir by the 1320s.</p>
<p>On the right bank of the Moskva River, at a distance of five miles (8.0 kilometres) from the Kremlin, not later than in 1282, Daniel founded the first monastery with the wooden church of St. Daniel-Stylite. Now it is the Danilov Monastery. Daniel died in 1303, at the age of 42. Before his death he became a monk and, according to his will, was buried in the cemetery of the St. Daniel Monastery.</p>
<p>Moscow was stable and prosperous for many years and attracted a large numbers of refugees from across Russia. The Rurikids maintained large landholdings by practicing <a href="/wiki/Primogeniture" title="Primogeniture">primogeniture</a>, whereby all land was passed to the eldest sons, rather than dividing it up among all sons. By 1304, <a href="/wiki/Yury_of_Moscow" title="Yury of Moscow">Yury of Moscow</a> contested with <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_of_Tver" title="Mikhail of Tver">Mikhail of Tver</a> for the throne of the principality of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir,_Russia" title="Vladimir, Russia">Vladimir</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ivan_I_of_Russia" title="Ivan I of Russia">Ivan I</a> eventually defeated <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">Tver</a> to become the sole collector of taxes for the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol" title="Mongol">Mongol</a> rulers, making Moscow the capital of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir-Suzdal" title="Vladimir-Suzdal">Vladimir-Suzdal</a>. By paying high tribute, Ivan won an important concession from the <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khan</a>.</p>
<p>While Khan of the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a> initially attempted to limit Moscow's influence, when the growth of the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Duchy_of_Lithuania" title="Grand Duchy of Lithuania">Grand Duchy of Lithuania</a> began to threaten all of Russia, the Khan strengthened Moscow to counterbalance Lithuania, allowing it to become one of the most powerful cities in Russia. In 1380, prince <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Donskoy" title="Dmitry Donskoy">Dmitry Donskoy</a> of Moscow led a united Russian army to an important victory over the Mongols in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kulikovo" title="Battle of Kulikovo">Battle of Kulikovo</a>. Afterwards, Moscow took the leading role in liberating Russia from Mongol domination. In 1480, <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III</a> had <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_standing_on_the_Ugra_river" title="Great standing on the Ugra river">finally broken the Russians free</a> from Tatar control, and Moscow became the capital of an empire that would eventually encompass all of Russia and <a href="/wiki/Siberia" title="Siberia">Siberia</a>, and parts of many other lands.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3068" data-file-width="2187" height="281" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg/200px-Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg/300px-Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg/400px-Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ivan_III_of_Russia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Ivan III</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1462 <a href="/wiki/Ivan_III_of_Russia" title="Ivan III of Russia">Ivan III, known as Ivan the Great</a> (1440–1505) became Grand Prince of Moscow (then part of the medieval Muscovy state). He began fighting the Tatars, enlarged the territory of Muscovy, and enriched his capital city. By 1500 it had a population of 100,000 and was one of the largest cities in the world. He conquered the far larger principality of Novgorod to the north, which had been allied to the hostile Lithuanians. Thus he enlarged the territory sevenfold, from 430,000 to 2,800,000 square kilometres (170,000 to 1,080,000 square miles). He took control of the ancient "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novgorod_Chronicle" title="Novgorod Chronicle">Novgorod Chronicle</a>" and made it a propaganda vehicle for his regime.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<p>The original <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a> was built during the 14th century. It was reconstructed by Ivan, who in the 1480s invited architects from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Italy" title="Renaissance Italy">Renaissance Italy</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Petrus_Antonius_Solarius" title="Petrus Antonius Solarius">Petrus Antonius Solarius</a>, who designed the new Kremlin wall and its towers, and <a href="/wiki/Marco_Ruffo" title="Marco Ruffo">Marco Ruffo</a> who designed the new palace for the prince. The Kremlin walls as they now appear are those designed by Solarius, completed in 1495. The Kremlin's Great Bell Tower was built in 1505–08 and augmented to its present height in 1600.</p>
<p>A trading settlement, or <i>posad</i>, grew up to the east of the Kremlin, in the area known as <i>Zaradye</i> (Зарядье). In the time of Ivan III, the <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a>, originally named the Hollow Field (Полое поле) appeared.</p>
<p>In 1508–1516, the Italian architect <a href="/wiki/Aloisio_the_New" title="Aloisio the New">Aleviz Fryazin (Novy)</a> arranged for the construction of a moat in front of the eastern wall, which would connect the <a href="/wiki/Moskva_River" title="Moskva River">Moskva</a> and <a href="/wiki/Neglinnaya_River" title="Neglinnaya River">Neglinnaya</a> and be filled in with water from Neglinnaya. This moat, known as the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alevizov_moat&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alevizov moat (page does not exist)">Alevizov moat</a> and having a length of 541 metres (1,775 feet), width of 36 metres (118 feet), and a depth of 9.5 to 13 metres (31–43 feet) was lined with limestone and, in 1533, fenced on both sides with low, four-metre-thick (13-foot) cogged-brick walls.</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<p><span id="Tsardom"></span></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Tsardom_.281547.E2.80.931721.29">Tsardom (1547–1721)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Tsardom (1547–1721)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Tsardom" title="Russian Tsardom">Russian Tsardom</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A0%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F._%D0%92%D1%81%D0%B5%D1%85%D1%81%D0%B2%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8C_%D0%B2_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%86%D0%B5_XVII_%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%B0._1922,_%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5,_%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8C,_%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C,_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%88.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="2035" height="141" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%D0%A0%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F._%D0%92%D1%81%D0%B5%D1%85%D1%81%D0%B2%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8C_%D0%B2_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%86%D0%B5_XVII_%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%B0._1922%2C_%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%2C_%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%88.jpg/220px-thumbnail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%D0%A0%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F._%D0%92%D1%81%D0%B5%D1%85%D1%81%D0%B2%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8C_%D0%B2_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%86%D0%B5_XVII_%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%B0._1922%2C_%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%2C_%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%88.jpg/330px-thumbnail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/69/%D0%A0%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F._%D0%92%D1%81%D0%B5%D1%85%D1%81%D0%B2%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8C_%D0%B2_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%86%D0%B5_XVII_%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%B0._1922%2C_%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5%2C_%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C%2C_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%88.jpg/440px-thumbnail.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A0%D0%B0%D1%81%D1%86%D0%B2%D0%B5%D1%82_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8F._%D0%92%D1%81%D0%B5%D1%85%D1%81%D0%B2%D1%8F%D1%82%D1%81%D0%BA%D0%B8%D0%B9_%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D1%82_%D0%B8_%D0%9A%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BC%D0%BB%D1%8C_%D0%B2_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%86%D0%B5_XVII_%D0%B2%D0%B5%D0%BA%D0%B0._1922,_%D0%B1%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B0%D0%B3%D0%B0_%D0%BD%D0%B0_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D1%82%D0%BE%D0%BD%D0%B5,_%D1%83%D0%B3%D0%BE%D0%BB%D1%8C,_%D0%B0%D0%BA%D0%B2%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8C,_%D0%BA%D0%B0%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BD%D0%B4%D0%B0%D1%88.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
View of 17th-century Moscow (1922 drawing by <a href="/wiki/Apollinary_Vasnetsov" title="Apollinary Vasnetsov">Apollinary Vasnetsov</a>)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 16th and 17th centuries, the three circular defenses were built: <a href="/wiki/Kitay-gorod" title="Kitay-gorod">Kitay-gorod</a> (Китай-город), the White City (Белый город) and the Earthen City (Земляной город). However, in 1547, two fires destroyed much of the town, and in 1571 the <a href="/wiki/Crimean_Tatars" title="Crimean Tatars">Crimean Tatars</a> <a href="/wiki/Russo-Crimean_Wars" title="Russo-Crimean Wars">captured Moscow</a>, burning everything except the Kremlin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> The annals record that only 30,000 of 200,000 inhabitants survived.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khanate_of_Crimea" title="Khanate of Crimea">Crimean Tatars</a> attacked again in 1591, but this time were held back by new defense walls, built between 1584 and 1591 by a craftsman named <a href="/wiki/Fyodor_Kon" title="Fyodor Kon">Fyodor Kon</a>. In 1592, an outer earth rampart with 50 towers was erected around the city, including an area on the right bank of the Moscow River. As an outermost line of defense, a chain of strongly fortified monasteries was established beyond the ramparts to the south and east, principally the <a href="/wiki/Novodevichy_Convent" title="Novodevichy Convent">Novodevichy Convent</a> and <a href="/wiki/Donskoy_Monastery" title="Donskoy Monastery">Donskoy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danilov_Monastery" title="Danilov Monastery">Danilov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Simonov_Monastery" title="Simonov Monastery">Simonov</a>, <a href="/wiki/Novospassky_Monastery" title="Novospassky Monastery">Novospasskiy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Andronikov_Monastery" title="Andronikov Monastery">Andronikov</a> monasteries, most of which now house museums. From its ramparts, the city became poetically known as <i>Bielokamennaya</i>, the "White-Walled". The limits of the city as marked by the ramparts built in 1592 are now marked by the <a href="/wiki/Garden_Ring" title="Garden Ring">Garden Ring</a>.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3200" data-file-width="2000" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg/220px-Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg/330px-Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg/440px-Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Basil-cathedral-morning.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil's Cathedral">Saint Basil's Cathedral</a>, 1561</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Three square gates existed on the eastern side of the Kremlin wall, which in the 17th century, were known as: Konstantino-Eleninsky, Spassky, Nikolsky (owing their names to the icons of Constantine and Helen, the Savior and St. Nicholas which hung over them). The last two were directly opposite the Red Square, while the Konstantino-Elenensky gate was located behind Saint Basil's Cathedral.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Russian_famine_of_1601%E2%80%9303" title="Russian famine of 1601–03">Russian famine of 1601–03</a> killed perhaps 100,000 in Moscow. From 1610 through 1612, troops of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polish-Lithuanian_Commonwealth" title="Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth">Polish-Lithuanian Commonwealth</a> occupied Moscow, as its ruler <a href="/wiki/Sigismund_III_Vasa" title="Sigismund III Vasa">Sigismund III</a> tried to take the Russian throne. In 1612, the people of <a href="/wiki/Nizhny_Novgorod" title="Nizhny Novgorod">Nizhny Novgorod</a> and other Russian cities conducted by prince <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Pozharsky" title="Dmitry Pozharsky">Dmitry Pozharsky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuzma_Minin" title="Kuzma Minin">Kuzma Minin</a> rose against the Polish occupants, <a href="/wiki/Time_of_Troubles" title="Time of Troubles">besieged the Kremlin, and expelled them</a>. In 1613, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zemsky_sobor" title="Zemsky sobor">Zemsky sobor</a> elected <a href="/wiki/Michael_I_of_Russia" title="Michael I of Russia">Michael Romanov</a> tsar, establishing the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romanov_dynasty" title="Romanov dynasty">Romanov dynasty</a>. The 17th century was rich in popular risings, such as the liberation of Moscow from the Polish–Lithuanian invaders (1612), the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salt_Riot" title="Salt Riot">Salt Riot</a> (1648), the <a href="/wiki/Copper_Riot" title="Copper Riot">Copper Riot</a> (1662), and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_Uprising_of_1682" title="Moscow Uprising of 1682">Moscow Uprising of 1682</a>.</p>
<p>During the first half of the 17th century, the population of Moscow doubled from roughly 100,000 to 200,000. It expanded beyond its ramparts in the later 17th century. By 1682, there were 692 households established north of the ramparts, by <a href="/wiki/Ukrainians" title="Ukrainians">Ukrainians</a> and <a href="/wiki/Belarusians" title="Belarusians">Belarusians</a> abducted from their hometowns in the course of <a href="/wiki/Russo-Polish_War_(1654%E2%80%931667)" title="Russo-Polish War (1654–1667)">Russo-Polish War (1654–1667)</a>. These new outskirts of the city came to be known as the <i>Meshchanskaya <a href="/wiki/Sloboda" title="Sloboda">sloboda</a></i>, after Ruthenian <i>meshchane</i> "town people". The term <i>meshchane</i> (мещане) acquired pejorative connotations in 18th-century Russia and today means "petty bourgeois" or "narrow-minded philistine".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<p>The entire city of the late 17th century, including the <a href="/wiki/Sloboda" title="Sloboda">slobodas</a> which grew up outside of the city ramparts, are contained within what is today Moscow's <a href="/wiki/Central_Administrative_Okrug" title="Central Administrative Okrug">Central Administrative Okrug</a>.</p>
<p>Numerous disasters befell the city. The <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">plague</a> epidemics ravaged Moscow in 1570–1571, 1592 and 1654–1656.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> The plague killed upwards of 80% of the people in 1654–55. Fires burned out much of the wooden city in 1626 and 1648.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Empire_.281721.E2.80.931917.29">Empire (1721–1917)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Empire (1721–1917)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Governorate" title="Moscow Governorate">Moscow Governorate</a></div>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Russian_Empire" title="Russian Empire">Russian Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tnone" style="clear:both">
<div class="thumbinner">
<div class="overflowbugx" style="overflow: auto;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg"><img alt="A panoramic view of Moscow in 1867." data-file-height="600" data-file-width="12569" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/3666px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/5510px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg/7332px-1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg 2x" width="3666"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg" title="File:1867 Moscow panorama megapanorama.jpg"></a></div>
<center>A <a href="/wiki/Panorama" title="Panorama">panoramic</a> view of Moscow in 1867. (<a class="extiw" href="https://commons.wikimedia.org/wiki/File:1867_Moscow_panorama_megapanorama.jpg" title="commons:File:1867 Moscow panorama megapanorama.jpg">Image with notes</a>).</center>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_(Russian_empire).png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="231" data-file-width="200" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_%28Russian_empire%29.png/125px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_%28Russian_empire%29.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_%28Russian_empire%29.png/188px-Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_%28Russian_empire%29.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_%28Russian_empire%29.png 2x" width="125"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Coat_of_Arms_of_Moscow_gubernia_(Russian_empire).png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The coat of arms of <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Governorate" title="Moscow Governorate">Moscow Governorate</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:267px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moskva_riverfront.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2323" data-file-width="3183" height="193" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Moskva_riverfront.jpg/265px-Moskva_riverfront.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Moskva_riverfront.jpg/398px-Moskva_riverfront.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/63/Moskva_riverfront.jpg/530px-Moskva_riverfront.jpg 2x" width="265"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moskva_riverfront.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Moskva riverside in the 19th century.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moscow ceased to be Russia's capital (except for a brief period from 1728 to 1732 under the influence of the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Privy_Council" title="Supreme Privy Council">Supreme Privy Council</a>) when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Peter_I_of_Russia" title="Peter I of Russia">Peter the Great</a> moved his government to the newly built <a href="/wiki/Saint_Petersburg" title="Saint Petersburg">Saint Petersburg</a> on the Baltic coast in 1712.</p>
<p>After losing the status as capital of the empire, the population of Moscow at first decreased, from 200,000 in the 17th century to 130,000 in 1750. But after 1750, the population grew more than tenfold over the remaining duration of the Russian Empire, reaching 1.8 million by 1915.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kniznije_lavki.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1300" data-file-width="1738" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/220px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/330px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Kniznije_lavki.jpg/440px-Kniznije_lavki.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kniznije_lavki.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Book shops at the Spassky bridge. By <a href="/wiki/Apollinary_Vasnetsov" title="Apollinary Vasnetsov">Apollinary Vasnetsov</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>By 1700, the building of cobbled roads had begun. In November 1730, the permanent street light was introduced, and by 1867 many streets had a gaslight. In 1883, near the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Prechistinskiye_Gates&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Prechistinskiye Gates (page does not exist)">Prechistinskiye Gates</a>, arc lamps were installed. In 1741 Moscow was surrounded by a barricade 25 miles (40 kilometres) long, the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Kamer-Kollezhskiy_barrier&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Kamer-Kollezhskiy barrier (page does not exist)">Kamer-Kollezhskiy barrier</a>, with 16 gates at which customs tolls were collected. Its line is traced today by a number of streets called <i>val</i> (“ramparts”). Between 1781–1804 the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Mytischinskiy_water-pipe&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Mytischinskiy water-pipe (page does not exist)">Mytischinskiy water-pipe</a> (the first in Russia) was built. In 1813 a Commission for the Construction of the City of Moscow was established. It launched a great program of rebuilding, including a partial replanning of the city-center. Among many buildings constructed or reconstructed at this time were the <a href="/wiki/Grand_Kremlin_Palace" title="Grand Kremlin Palace">Grand Kremlin Palace</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kremlin_Armoury" title="Kremlin Armoury">Kremlin Armoury</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moscow_University" title="Moscow University">Moscow University</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Manege" title="Moscow Manege">Moscow Manege</a> (Riding School), and the <a href="/wiki/Bolshoi_Theatre" title="Bolshoi Theatre">Bolshoi Theatre</a>. In 1903 the <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Moskvoretskaya_water-supply&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Moskvoretskaya water-supply (page does not exist)">Moskvoretskaya water-supply</a> was completed.</p>
<p>In the early 19th century, the Arch of Konstantino-Elenensky gate was paved with bricks, but the Spassky Gate was the main front gate of the Kremlin and used for royal entrances. From this gate, wooden and (following the 17th-century improvements) stone bridges stretched across the moat. Books were sold on this bridge and stone platforms were built nearby for guns – "raskats". The <a href="/wiki/Tsar_Cannon" title="Tsar Cannon">Tsar Cannon</a> was located on the platform of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lobnoye_mesto" title="Lobnoye mesto">Lobnoye mesto</a>.</p>
<p>The road connecting Moscow with St. Petersburg, now the <a href="/wiki/M10_highway_(Russia)" title="M10 highway (Russia)">M10 highway</a>, was completed in 1746, its Moscow end following the old <a href="/wiki/Tver" title="Tver">Tver</a> road which had existed since the 16th century. It became known as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningradsky_Prospekt" title="Leningradsky Prospekt">Peterburskoye Schosse</a></i> after it was paved in the 1780s. <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Petrovsky_Palace&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Petrovsky Palace (page does not exist)">Petrovsky Palace</a> was built in 1776–1780 by <a href="/wiki/Matvey_Kazakov" title="Matvey Kazakov">Matvey Kazakov</a> as a railway station specifically reserved for royal journeys from Saint Petersburg to Moscow, while coaches for lesser classes arrived and departed from <a href="/wiki/Vsekhsvyatskoye" title="Vsekhsvyatskoye">Vsekhsvyatskoye</a> station.</p>
<p>When <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon_I_of_France" title="Napoleon I of France">Napoleon</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Napoleon%27s_invasion_of_Russia" title="Napoleon's invasion of Russia">invaded</a> Russia in 1812, the Moscovites were evacuated. It is suspected that the <a href="/wiki/Fire_of_Moscow_(1812)" title="Fire of Moscow (1812)">Moscow fire</a> was principally the effect of Russian sabotage. Napoleon’s <i><a href="/wiki/Grande_Arm%C3%A9e" title="Grande Armée">Grande Armée</a></i> was forced to retreat and was nearly annihilated by the devastating Russian winter and sporadic attacks by Russian military forces. As many as 400,000 of Napoleon's soldiers died during this time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:212px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chumbunt.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="400" height="159" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Chumbunt.png/210px-Chumbunt.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Chumbunt.png/315px-Chumbunt.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/Chumbunt.png 2x" width="210"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chumbunt.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Moscow_plague_riot_of_1771" title="Moscow plague riot of 1771">Moscow plague riot of 1771</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a> was established in 1755. Its main building was reconstructed after the 1812 fire by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domenico_Giliardi" title="Domenico Giliardi">Domenico Giliardi</a>. The <i><a href="/wiki/Moskovskiye_Vedomosti" title="Moskovskiye Vedomosti">Moskovskiye Vedomosti</a></i> newspaper appeared from 1756, originally in weekly intervals, and from 1859 as a daily newspaper.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Arbat_Street" title="Arbat Street">Arbat Street</a> had been in existence since at least the 15th century, but it was developed into a prestigious area during the 18th century. It was destroyed in the fire of 1812 and was rebuilt completely in the early 19th century.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="428" data-file-width="600" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg/220px-Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg/330px-Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg/440px-Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Fire_of_Moscow_1812.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/French_invasion_of_Russia" title="French invasion of Russia">French invasion of Russia</a> in 1812, <a href="/wiki/Fire_of_Moscow_(1812)" title="Fire of Moscow (1812)">Fire of Moscow</a>, painting by A.F. Smirnov 1813</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the 1830s, general <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Bashilov" title="Alexander Bashilov">Alexander Bashilov</a> planned the first regular grid of city streets north from Petrovsky Palace. <a href="/wiki/Khodynka_Field" title="Khodynka Field">Khodynka</a> field south of the highway was used for military training. Smolensky Rail station (forerunner of present-day <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Belorussky_Rail_Terminal" title="Belorussky Rail Terminal">Belorussky Rail Terminal</a>) was inaugurated in 1870. <a href="/wiki/Sokolniki_Park" title="Sokolniki Park">Sokolniki Park</a>, in the 18th century the home of the tsar's falconers well outside of Moscow, became contiguous with the expanding city in the later 19th century and was developed into a public municipal park in 1878. The suburban <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savyolovsky_Rail_Terminal" title="Savyolovsky Rail Terminal">Savyolovsky Rail Terminal</a> was built in 1902. In January 1905, the institution of the City Governor, or <a href="/wiki/Mayor" title="Mayor">Mayor</a>, was officially introduced in Moscow, and <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Alexander_Adrianov&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Alexander Adrianov (page does not exist)">Alexander Adrianov</a> became Moscow's first official mayor.</p>
<p>When <a href="/wiki/Catherine_the_Great" title="Catherine the Great">Catherine II</a> came to power in 1762, the city's filth and smell of sewage was depicted by observers as a symptom of disorderly life styles of lower-class Russians recently arrived from the farms. Elites called for improving sanitation, which became part of Catherine's plans for increasing control over social life. National political and military successes from 1812 through 1855 calmed the critics and validated efforts to produce a more enlightened and stable society. There was less talk about the smell and the poor conditions of public health. However, in the wake of Russia's failures in the Crimean War in 1855–56, confidence in the ability of the state to maintain order in the slums eroded, and demands for improved public health put filth back on the agenda.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Soviet_era_.281917.E2.80.931991.29">Soviet era (1917–1991)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Soviet era (1917–1991)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Russian_Soviet_Federative_Socialist_Republic" title="Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic">Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<p>Following the success of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Revolution_of_1917" title="Russian Revolution of 1917">Russian Revolution of 1917</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Lenin" title="Vladimir Lenin">Vladimir Lenin</a>, fearing possible foreign invasion, moved the capital from Saint Petersburg back to Moscow on March 5, 1918. The Kremlin once again became the seat of power and the political centre of the new state.</p>
<p>During the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Great_Patriotic_War" title="Great Patriotic War">Great Patriotic War</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/USSR" title="USSR">Soviet</a> State Committee of Defense and the General Staff of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Army" title="Red Army">Red Army</a> were located in Moscow. In 1941, 16 divisions of the national volunteers (more than 160,000 people), 25 battalions (18,000 people) and 4 engineering regiments were formed among the Muscovites. In November 1941, German <a href="/wiki/Army_Group_Centre" title="Army Group Centre">Army Group Centre</a> was stopped at the outskirts of the city and then driven off in the course of the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Moscow" title="Battle of Moscow">Battle of Moscow</a>. Many factories were evacuated, together with much of the government, and from October 20 the city was declared to be in a state of siege. Its remaining inhabitants built and manned antitank defenses, while the city was bombarded from the air. On May 1, 1944 a medal "For the defense of Moscow" and in 1947 another medal "In memory of the 800th anniversary of Moscow" were instituted.</p>
<p>Both <a href="/wiki/German_casualties_in_World_War_II" title="German casualties in World War II">German</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_casualties_of_the_Soviet_Union" title="World War II casualties of the Soviet Union">Soviet casualties</a> during the battle of Moscow have been a subject of debate, as various sources provide somewhat different estimates. Total casualties between 30 September 1941, and 7 January 1942, are estimated to be between 248,000 and 400,000 for the <a href="/wiki/Wehrmacht" title="Wehrmacht">Wehrmacht</a> and between 650,000 and 1,280,000 for the Red Army.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>During the postwar years, there was a serious housing crisis, solved by the invention of <a href="/wiki/Tower_block" title="Tower block">high-rise apartments</a>. There are over 11,000 of these standardized and prefabricated apartment blocks, housing the majority of Moscow's population, making it by far the city with the most high-rise buildings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hrranking_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-hrranking-32">[32]</a></sup> Apartments were built and partly furnished in the factory before being raised and stacked into tall columns. The popular Soviet-era comic film <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irony_of_Fate" title="Irony of Fate">Irony of Fate</a></i> parodies this construction method.</p>
<p>The city of <a href="/wiki/Zelenograd" title="Zelenograd">Zelenograd</a> was built in 1958 at 37 kilometres (23 miles) from the city center to the north-west, along the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leningradskoye_Shosse" title="Leningradskoye Shosse">Leningradskoye Shosse</a>, and incorporated as one of Moscow's <a href="/wiki/Administrative_divisions_of_Moscow" title="Administrative divisions of Moscow">administrative okrugs</a>. <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_University" title="Moscow State University">Moscow State University</a> moved to its campus on <a href="/wiki/Sparrow_Hills" title="Sparrow Hills">Sparrow Hills</a> in 1953.</p>
<p>On May 8, 1965 due to the actual 20th <a href="/wiki/Victory_Day_(9_May)" title="Victory Day (9 May)">anniversary of the victory</a> in <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> Moscow was awarded a title of the <a href="/wiki/Hero_City" title="Hero City">Hero City</a>. In 1980 it hosted the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">Summer Olympic Games</a>.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MKAD" title="MKAD">MKAD</a> ring road was opened in 1961. It had four lanes running 109 kilometres (68 miles) along the city borders. The MKAD marked the administrative boundaries of the city of Moscow until the 1980s, when outlying suburbs beyond the ring road began to be incorporated. In 1980, it hosted the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">Summer Olympic Games</a>, which were boycotted by the <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">United States</a> and several other Western countries due to the Soviet Union's involvement in <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> in late 1979. In 1991 Moscow was the scene of a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Soviet_coup_attempt_of_1991" title="Soviet coup attempt of 1991">coup attempt</a> by conservators opposed to the <a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">liberal reforms</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Metro">Metro</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Metro">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3456" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg/220px-Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg/330px-Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg/440px-Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mayakovskaya_after_renewing_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Mayakovskaya_(Moscow_Metro)" title="Mayakovskaya (Moscow Metro)">Mayakovskaya station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3334" data-file-width="5000" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg/220px-Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg/330px-Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg/440px-Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Metro_MSK_Line2_Novokuznetskaya.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Novokuznetskaya" title="Novokuznetskaya">Novokuznetskaya station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Metro" title="Moscow Metro">Moscow Metro</a> opened in 1935 and immediately became the centerpiece of the transportation system. More than that it was a Stalinist device to awe and reward the populace, and give them an appreciation of Soviet realist art. It became the prototype for future Soviet large-scale technologies. <a href="/wiki/Lazar_Kaganovich" title="Lazar Kaganovich">Lazar Kaganovich</a> was in charge; he designed the subway so that citizens would absorb the values and ethos of Stalinist civilization as they rode. The artwork of the 13 original stations became nationally and internationally famous. For example, the Sverdlov Square subway station featured porcelain bas-reliefs depicting the daily life of the Soviet peoples, and the bas-reliefs at the Dynamo Stadium sports complex glorified sports and the physical prowess of the powerful new "Homo Sovieticus." (Soviet man).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The metro was touted as the symbol of the new social order—a sort of Communist cathedral of engineering modernity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Soviet workers did the labor and the art work, but the main engineering designs, routes, and construction plans were handled by specialists recruited from the London Underground. The Britons called for tunneling instead of the "cut-and-cover" technique, the use of escalators instead of lifts, and designed the routes and the rolling stock.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The paranoia of Stalin and the NKVD was evident when the secret police arrested numerous British engineers for espionage—that is for gaining an in-depth knowledge of the city's physical layout. Engineers for the Metropolitan Vickers Electrical Company were given a show trial and deported in 1933, ending the role of British business in the USSR.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recent_history_.281991_to_present.29">Recent history (1991 to present)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Recent history (1991 to present)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2736" data-file-width="3648" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG/220px-%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG/330px-%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c0/%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG/440px-%D0%A4%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%82%D0%B0%D0%BD%D1%8B_%D1%86%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82%D1%80%D0%B0%D0%BB%D1%8C%D0%BD%D0%BE%D0%B9_%D0%B0%D0%BB%D0%BB%D0%B5%D0%B8_%D0%92%D0%94%D0%9D%D0%A5_02.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>

<p>When the USSR was dissolved in the same year, Moscow remained the capital of the Russian SFSR (on December 25, 1991 the Russian SFSR was renamed the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Russian_Federation" title="Russian Federation">Russian Federation</a>). Since then a market economy has emerged in Moscow, producing an explosion of Western-style retailing, services, architecture, and lifestyles.</p>
<p>The city has continued to grow during the 1990s to 2000s, its population rising from below nine to above ten million. Mason and Nigmatullina argue that Soviet-era urban-growth controls (before 1991) produced controlled and sustainable metropolitan development, typified by the greenbelt built in 1935. Since then however, there has been a dramatic growth of low-density suburban sprawl, created by a heavy demand for single-family dwellings as opposed to crowded apartments. In 1995–1997 the MKAD ring road was widened from the initial four to ten lanes. In December 2002 <a href="/wiki/Bulvar_Dmitriya_Donskogo" title="Bulvar Dmitriya Donskogo">Bulvar Dmitriya Donskogo</a> became the first Moscow Metro station that opened beyond the limits of MKAD. The <a href="/wiki/Third_Ring_Road_(Moscow)" title="Third Ring Road (Moscow)">Third Ring Road</a>, intermediate between the early 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Garden_Ring" title="Garden Ring">Garden Ring</a> and the Soviet-era outer ring road, was completed in 2004. The greenbelt is becoming more and more fragmented, and satellite cities are appearing at the fringe. Summer dachas are being converted into year-round residences, and with the proliferation of automobiles there is heavy traffic congestion.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Multiple old churches and other examples of architectural heritage that had been demolished during the Stalin era have been restored, such as <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography_and_climate">Geography and climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Geography and climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Location">Location</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Location">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="1000" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg/170px-Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg/255px-Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg/340px-Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_satellite_image.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3004" data-file-width="3750" height="176" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG/220px-Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG/330px-Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/93/Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG/440px-Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_aerial_view_looking_towards_the_south-east.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Moscow aerial view</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moscow is situated on the banks of the <a href="/wiki/Moskva_River" title="Moskva River">Moskva River</a>, which flows for just over 500 km (311 mi) through the <a href="/wiki/East_European_Plain" title="East European Plain">East European Plain</a> in central Russia. <a href="/wiki/List_of_bridges_in_Moscow" title="List of bridges in Moscow">49 bridges</a> span the river and its canals within the city's limits. The <a href="/wiki/Elevation" title="Elevation">elevation</a> of Moscow at the All-Russia Exhibition Center (VVC), where the leading Moscow weather station is situated, is 156 metres (512 feet). Teplostanskaya highland is the city's highest point at 255 metres (837 feet).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> The width of Moscow city (not limiting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MKAD" title="MKAD">MKAD</a>) from west to east is 39.7 km (24.7 mi), and the length from north to south is 51.8 km (32.2 mi).</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Time">Time</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Time">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Time" title="Moscow Time">Moscow Time</a></div>
<p>Moscow serves as the reference point for the timezone used in most of <a href="/wiki/European_Russia" title="European Russia">European Russia</a>, including Saint Petersburg, Belarus, and the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Crimea" title="Republic of Crimea">Republic of Crimea</a>. The areas operate in what is referred to in international standards as <i>Moscow Standard Time (MSK, мск)</i>, which is 3 hours ahead of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC" title="UTC">UTC</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UTC%2B3" title="UTC+3">UTC+3</a>. <a href="/wiki/Daylight_saving_time" title="Daylight saving time">Daylight saving time</a> is no longer observed.</p>
<div class="legend"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #a5ffa5; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;"> </span> Moscow Time (UTC+3)</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate">Climate</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Climate">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Climate_of_Moscow" title="Climate of Moscow">Climate of Moscow</a></div>
<p>Moscow has a <a href="/wiki/Hemiboreal" title="Hemiboreal">hemiboreal climate</a> (<a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="Köppen climate classification">Köppen climate classification</a> <i>Dfb</i>) with long, cold (although average by Russian standards) winters usually lasting from mid-November through the end of March, and warm summers. Weather can fluctuate widely with temperatures ranging from −25 °C (−13 °F) in the city and −30 °C (−22 °F) in suburbs to above 5 °C (41 °F) in the winter, and from 10 to 35 °C (50 to 95 °F) in the summer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup></p>
<p>Typical high temperatures in the warm months of June, July and August are around a comfortable 20 to 26 °C (68 to 79 °F), but during heat waves (which can occur between May and September), daytime high temperatures often exceed 30 °C (86 °F), sometimes for a week or two at a time. In the winter, average temperatures normally drop to approximately −10 °C (14 °F), though almost every winter there are periods of warmth with day temperatures rising above 0 °C (32 °F), and periods of cooling with night temperatures falling below −30 °C (−22 °F). These periods usually last about a week or two.</p>
<p>The highest temperature ever recorded was 38.2 °C (100.8 °F)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> at the <a href="/wiki/VVC_weather_station" title="VVC weather station">VVC weather station</a> and 39.0 °C (102.2 °F) in the center of Moscow and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Domodedovo_airport" title="Domodedovo airport">Domodedovo airport</a> on July 29, 2010 during the unusual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_Northern_Hemisphere_summer_heat_wave" title="2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat wave">2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat wave</a>. Record high temperatures were recorded for January, March, April, May, July, August, November and December in 2007 – 2014.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pogoda_.28data.29_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-pogoda_.28data.29-41">[41]</a></sup> The average July temperature from 1981–2010 is 19.2 °C (66.6 °F). The lowest ever recorded temperature was −42.2 °C (−44.0 °F) in January 1940. Snow, which is present for about five months a year, often begins to fall mid October, while snow cover lies in November and melts in the beginning of April.</p>
<p>On average Moscow has 1731 hours of sunshine per year, varying from a low of 8% in December to 52% from May to August.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> Between 2004 and 2010, the average was between 1800 and 2000 hours with a tendency to more sunshine in summer months, up to record 411 hours in July 2014, 79% of possible sunshine.</p>
<p>Temperatures in the centre of Moscow are often significantly higher than in the outskirts and nearby suburbs, especially in winter. For example, if the average February temperature in the north-east of Moscow is −6.7 °C (19.9 °F), in the suburbs it's about −9 °C (16 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> The temperature difference between the centre of Moscow and nearby areas of <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Oblast" title="Moscow Oblast">Moscow Oblast</a> can sometimes be more than 10 °C (18 °F) on frosty winter nights.</p>
