<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Climate_changes">Climate changes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Climate changes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Below is the 1961–1990 normals table. The annual temperature rose from 5.0 °C (41.0 °F) to 5.8 °C (42.4 °F) in the new 1981–2010 normals. In 2015, the average annual temperature reached a record of 7.5 °C (45.5 °F)</p>

</table>
<p>Moscow, being in the mid-latitudes of the northern hemisphere, is a place with the most expressed signs of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Thus the climate becomes less stable, but the average yearly temperature is growing. So, the period from July until the beginning of August has become considerably warmer. During these time periods of extreme heat are often observed in the city (2001, 2002, 2003, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/2010_Northern_Hemisphere_summer_heat_wave" title="2010 Northern Hemisphere summer heat wave">2010</a>, 2011). With a southern part of <a href="/wiki/Central_Federal_District" title="Central Federal District">Central Russia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> after few last years hot summer seasons, climate of city gets <a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate#Koppen_Dfa" title="Humid continental climate">dfa</a> trends. Winter also became significantly milder: for example, the average January temperature in the early 1900s was −12.0 °C (10.4 °F), while now it is about −7.0 °C (19.4 °F).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-weathernews1_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-weathernews1-54">[54]</a></sup> At the end of January–February it is often colder, with frosts reaching −30.0 °C (−22.0 °F) a few nights per year (2006, 2010, 2011, 2012, and 2013). The last decade was the warmest in the history of meteorological observations of Moscow. Temperature changes in the city are depicted in the table below:</p>
<table class="wikitable collapsible" style="width:90%; text-align:center; font-size:90%; line-height: 1.2em; margin:auto;">

<p>According to the results of the 2010 Census, the population of Moscow was 11,503,501;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_objectively_assess_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-2014_objectively_assess-56">[56]</a></sup> up from 10,382,754 recorded in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Census_(2002)" title="Russian Census (2002)">2002 Census</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2002Census_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-2002Census-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>At the time of the official 2010 Census, the ethnic makeup of the city's population whose ethnicity was known (10,835,092 people) was:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2014_objectively_assess_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-2014_objectively_assess-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>Russian: 9,930,410 (91.65%)</li>
<li>Ukrainian: 154,104 (1.42%)</li>
<li>Tatar: 149,043 (1.38%)</li>
<li>Armenian: 106,466 (0.98%)</li>
<li>Azeri: 57,123 (0.5%)</li>
<li>Belarusian: 39,225 (0.4%)</li>
<li>Georgian: 38,934 (0.4%)</li>
<li>Uzbek: 35,595 (0.3%)</li>
<li>Tajik: 27,280 (0.2%)</li>
<li>Moldovan: 21,699 (0.2%)</li>
<li>Mordvin: 17,095 (0.2%)</li>
<li>Chechen: 14,524 (0.1%)</li>
<li>Chuvash: 14,313 (0.1%)</li>
<li>Ossetian: 11,311 (0.1%)</li>
<li>Others: 164,825 (1.6%)</li>
<li>668,409 people were registered from administrative databases, and could not declare an ethnicity. It is estimated that the proportion of ethnicities in this group is the same as that of the declared group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<p>The official population of Moscow is based on those holding "<a href="/wiki/Resident_registration_in_Russia" title="Resident registration in Russia">permanent residency</a>." According to Russia's Federal Migration Service, Moscow holds 1.8 million official "guests" who have temporary residency on the basis of visas or other documentation, giving a legal population of 13.3 million. The number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Illegal_immigration_in_Russia" title="Illegal immigration in Russia">undocumented migrants</a>, the vast majority originating from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>, is estimated to be an additional 1 million people,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> giving a total population of about 14.3 million.</p>
<p>Total fertility rate:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<ul>
<li>2009 – 1.22</li>
<li>2010 – 1.25</li>
<li>2011 – 1.25</li>
<li>2012 – 1.32</li>
<li>2013 – 1.33</li>
<li>2014 – 1.34</li>
<li>2015 – 1.40
<ul>
<li>Births (2015): 142 390 (11.7 per 1000)</li>
<li>Deaths (2015): 121 954 (10.0 per 1000)</li>
</ul>
</li>
</ul>

<p><br/></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2770" data-file-width="3048" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg/220px-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg/330px-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg/440px-Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour-1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Saviour" title="Cathedral of Christ the Saviour">Cathedral of Christ the Saviour</a>, demolished during the Soviet period, was reconstructed during 1990–2000</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral,_preserved_part_no._3,_Donskoy_Monastery,_Moscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1224" data-file-width="1632" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg/220px-Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg/330px-Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg/440px-Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral%2C_preserved_part_no._3%2C_Donskoy_Monastery%2C_Moscow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Christ_the_Saviour_Cathedral,_preserved_part_no._3,_Donskoy_Monastery,_Moscow.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A preserved part of the original Christ the Saviour Cathedral</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><br/>
<a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christianity</a> is the predominant religion in the city, of which the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Orthodox_Church" title="Russian Orthodox Church">Russian Orthodox Church</a> is the most popular. Moscow is Russia's capital of <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodox_Church" title="Eastern Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox Christianity</a>, which has been the <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_Russia" title="Religion in Russia">country’s traditional religion</a> and was deemed a part of Russia's "historical heritage" in a law passed in 1997.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> Other religions practiced in Moscow include <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Apostolic_Church" title="Armenian Apostolic Church">Armenian Apostolicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Buddhism_in_Russia" title="Buddhism in Russia">Buddhism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hinduism_in_Russia" title="Hinduism in Russia">Hinduism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholicism_in_Russia" title="Roman Catholicism in Russia">Catholicism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Islam_in_Russia" title="Islam in Russia">Islam</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yazidism" title="Yazidism">Yazidism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Old_Believers" title="Old Believers">Old Believers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Protestantism_in_Russia" title="Protestantism in Russia">Protestantism</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rodnovery" title="Rodnovery">Rodnovery</a>.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Moscow_and_all_Rus%27" title="Patriarch of Moscow and all Rus'">Patriarch of Moscow</a> serves as the head of the church and resides in the <a href="/wiki/Danilov_Monastery" title="Danilov Monastery">Danilov Monastery</a>. Moscow was called the "city of 40 times 40 churches"—<i>"город сорока сороков церквей"</i>—prior to 1917. In 1918 the Bolshevik government declared Russia a <a href="/wiki/Secular_state" title="Secular state">secular state</a>, which in practice meant that religion <a href="/wiki/Persecution_of_Christians_in_the_Soviet_Union" title="Persecution of Christians in the Soviet Union">was repressed</a> and society was to become atheistic. During the period of 1920-1930s a <a href="/wiki/USSR_anti-religious_campaign_(1921%E2%80%9328)" title="USSR anti-religious campaign (1921–28)">great number of churches in Moscow were demolished</a>, including historical <a href="/wiki/Chudov_Monastery" title="Chudov Monastery">Chudov Monastery</a> in the Kremlin, dating from the 14th century, <a href="/wiki/Kazan_Cathedral,_Moscow" title="Kazan Cathedral, Moscow">Kazansky Cathedral</a> on the Red Square, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Savior" title="Cathedral of Christ the Savior">Cathedral of Christ the Savior</a>, constructed in the 19th century in memory of a victory over Napoleon's army in 1812, and many more. This continued even after the Second World War, in 1940-1970s, when persecutions against religion in the Soviet Union became less severe. Most of the surviving churches and monasteries were closed and then used as clubs, offices, factories or even warehouses. Since the disintegration of the Soviet Union in 1991 many of the destroyed churches have been restored and traditional religions are once again gaining popularity. Among the churches reconstructed in the 1990s is an impressive new Cathedral of Christ the Savior which once more has become a landmark. It was built on the site of the old demolished cathedral, where there had been a huge open swimming-pool until 1994.</p>
<p>The Moscow mufti council claimed that <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> numbered around 1.5 million of 10.5 millions of the city's population in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Spie_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-Spie-62">[62]</a></sup> There are four <a href="/wiki/Mosque" title="Mosque">mosques</a> in the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<p>Moscow Cathedral Mosque has been built at the site of the former one. It was officially inaugurated on September 23, 2015. The new mosque has the capacity of ten thousand worshippers. Presidents of Russia <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a>, of Turkey <a href="/wiki/Recep_Tayyip_Erdo%C4%9Fan" title="Recep Tayyip Erdoğan">Recep Tayyip Erdoğan</a>, of Palestinian territories <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Abbas" title="Mahmoud Abbas">Mahmoud Abbas</a> and local Muslim leaders participated in the inauguration ceremony of this mosque.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:RedSquare_SaintBasile_(pixinn.net).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1024" data-file-width="680" height="331" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/220px-RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/330px-RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg/440px-RedSquare_SaintBasile_%28pixinn.net%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<p>Moscow's architecture is world-renowned. Moscow is the site of <a href="/wiki/Saint_Basil%27s_Cathedral" title="Saint Basil's Cathedral">Saint Basil’s Cathedral</a>, with its elegant <a href="/wiki/Onion_dome" title="Onion dome">onion domes</a>, as well as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_Christ_the_Savior" title="Cathedral of Christ the Savior">Cathedral of Christ the Savior</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters</a>. The first Kremlin was built in the middle of the 12th century.</p>
<p>Medieval Moscow's design was of concentric walls and intersecting radial thoroughfares. This layout, as well as Moscow's rivers, helped shape Moscow's design in subsequent centuries.</p>
<p>The Kremlin was rebuilt in the 15th century. Its towers and some of its churches were built by Italian architects, lending the city some of the aura of the renaissance. From the end of the 15th century, the city was embellished by masonry structures such as monasteries, palaces, walls, towers, and churches.</p>
<p>The city's appearance had not changed much by the 18th century. Houses were made of pine and spruce logs, with shingled roofs plastered with sod or covered by birch bark. The rebuilding of Moscow in the second half of the 18th century was necessitated not only by constant fires, but also the needs of the nobility. Much of the wooden city was replaced by buildings in the classical style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>For much of its architectural history, Moscow was dominated by Orthodox churches. However, the overall appearance of the city changed drastically during Soviet times, especially as a result of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a>'s large-scale effort to "modernize" Moscow. Stalin's plans for the city included a network of broad avenues and roadways, some of them over ten lanes wide, which, while greatly simplifying movement through the city, were constructed at the expense of a great number of historical buildings and districts. Among the many casualties of Stalin's demolitions was the <a href="/wiki/Sukharev_Tower" title="Sukharev Tower">Sukharev Tower</a>, a longtime city landmark, as well as mansions and commercial buildings The city's newfound status as the capital of a deeply <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secular" title="Secular">secular</a> nation, made religiously significant buildings especially vulnerable to demolition. Many of the city's churches, which in most cases were some of Moscow's oldest and most prominent buildings, were destroyed; some notable examples include the <a href="/wiki/Kazan_Cathedral,_Moscow" title="Kazan Cathedral, Moscow">Kazan Cathedral</a> and the Cathedral of Christ the Savior. During the 1990s, both were rebuilt. Many smaller churches, however, were lost.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>While the later Stalinist period was characterized by the curtailing of creativity and architectural innovation, the earlier post-revolutionary years saw a plethora of radical new buildings created in the city. Especially notable were the constructivist architects associated with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/VKHUTEMAS" title="VKHUTEMAS">VKHUTEMAS</a>, responsible for such landmarks as Lenin's Mausoleum. Another prominent architect was <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Shukhov" title="Vladimir Shukhov">Vladimir Shukhov</a>, famous for Shukhov Tower, just one of many <a href="/wiki/Hyperboloid_structure" title="Hyperboloid structure">hyperboloid towers</a> designed by Shukhov. It was built between 1919 and 1922 as a transmission tower for a Russian broadcasting company.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stako_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-stako-68">[68]</a></sup> Shukhov also left a lasting legacy to the <a href="/wiki/Constructivism_(art)" title="Constructivism (art)">Constructivist</a> architecture of early Soviet Russia. He designed spacious elongated shop galleries, most notably the <a href="/wiki/GUM_(department_store)" title="GUM (department store)">GUM</a> department store on <a href="/wiki/Red_Square" title="Red Square">Red Square</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-stako_68-1"><a href="#cite_note-stako-68">[68]</a></sup> bridged with innovative metal-and-glass vaults.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ostankino_Tower,_2015.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3571" data-file-width="2384" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/220px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/330px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG/440px-Ostankino_Tower%2C_2015.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ostankino_Tower,_2015.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a>, the tallest freestanding structure in <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_freestanding_structures_in_the_world" title="List of tallest freestanding structures in the world">8th tallest in the world</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Perhaps the most recognizable contributions of the Stalinist period are the so-called <a href="/wiki/Seven_Sisters_(Moscow)" title="Seven Sisters (Moscow)">Seven Sisters</a>, comprising seven massive skyscrapers scattered throughout the city at about an equal distance from the Kremlin. A defining feature of Moscow’s skyline, their imposing form was allegedly inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Manhattan_Municipal_Building" title="Manhattan Municipal Building">Manhattan Municipal Building</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>, and their style—with intricate exteriors and a large central spire—has been described as <a href="/wiki/Stalinist_architecture" title="Stalinist architecture">Stalinist Gothic architecture</a>. All seven towers can be seen from most high points in the city; they are among the tallest constructions in central Moscow apart from the <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Tower" title="Ostankino Tower">Ostankino Tower</a>, which, when it was completed in 1967, was the highest free-standing land structure in the world and today remains the world’s seventy-second tallest, ranking among buildings such as the <a href="/wiki/Burj_Khalifa" title="Burj Khalifa">Burj Khalifa</a> in Dubai, <a href="/wiki/Taipei_101" title="Taipei 101">Taipei 101</a> in Taiwan and the <a href="/wiki/CN_Tower" title="CN Tower">CN Tower</a> in Toronto.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Soviet goal of providing housing for every family, and the rapid growth of Moscow's population, led to the construction of large, monotonous housing blocks. Most of these date from the post-Stalin era and the styles are often named after the leader then in power (Brezhnev, Khrushchev, etc.). They are usually badly maintained.</p>
<p>Although the city still has some five-story apartment buildings constructed before the mid-1960s, more recent apartment buildings are usually at least nine floors tall, and have <a href="/wiki/Elevator" title="Elevator">elevators</a>. It is estimated that Moscow has over twice as many elevators as New York City and four times as many as <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>. Moslift, one of the city's major elevator operating companies, has about 1500 elevator mechanics on call, to release residents trapped in elevators.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Stalinist_architecture" title="Stalinist architecture">Stalinist-era buildings</a>, mostly found in the central part of the city, are massive and usually ornamented with <a href="/wiki/Socialist_realism" title="Socialist realism">Socialist realism</a> motifs that imitate <a href="/wiki/Classical_architecture" title="Classical architecture">classical</a> themes. However, small churches – almost always <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Orthodox_Church" title="Orthodox Church">Eastern Orthodox</a>– found across the city provide glimpses of its past. The Old <a href="/wiki/Arbat_Street" title="Arbat Street">Arbat Street</a>, a tourist street that was once the heart of a bohemian area, preserves most of its buildings from prior to the 20th century. Many buildings found off the main streets of the inner city (behind the Stalinist <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fa%C3%A7ade" title="Façade">façades</a> of <a href="/wiki/Tverskaya_Street" title="Tverskaya Street">Tverskaya Street</a>, for example) are also examples of <a href="/wiki/Bourgeoisie" title="Bourgeoisie">bourgeois</a> architecture typical of Tsarist times. <a href="/wiki/Ostankino_Palace" title="Ostankino Palace">Ostankino Palace</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kuskovo" title="Kuskovo">Kuskovo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzkoye" title="Uzkoye">Uzkoye</a> and other large estates just outside Moscow originally belong to nobles from the Tsarist era, and some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template:Cloisters_of_Moscow" title="Template:Cloisters of Moscow">convents and monasteries</a>, both inside and outside the city, are open to Muscovites and tourists.</p>
<p>Attempts are being made to restore many of the city’s best-kept examples of pre-Soviet architecture. These restored structures are easily spotted by their bright new colors and spotless façades. There are a few examples of notable, early Soviet <a href="/wiki/Avant-garde" title="Avant-garde">avant-garde</a> work too, such as the house of the architect <a href="/wiki/Konstantin_Melnikov" title="Konstantin Melnikov">Konstantin Melnikov</a> in the Arbat area. Many of these restorations were criticized for alleged disrespect of historical authenticity. <a href="/wiki/Facadism" title="Facadism">Facadism</a> is also widely practiced.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> Later examples of interesting Soviet architecture are usually marked by their impressive size and the semi-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Modernist" title="Modernist">Modernist</a> styles employed, such as with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Novy_Arbat" title="Novy Arbat">Novy Arbat</a> project, familiarly known as "false teeth of Moscow" and notorious for the wide-scale disruption of a historic area in central Moscow involved in the project.</p>
<div class="thumb tmulti tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:373px;max-width:373px">
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:172px;max-width:172px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_(14006733426).jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="4300" data-file-width="2800" height="261" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg/170px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg/255px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg/340px-%D0%9C%D0%BE%D0%BD%D1%83%D0%BC%D0%B5%D0%BD%D1%82_%C2%AB%D0%9F%D0%BE%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%80%D0%B8%D1%82%D0%B5%D0%BB%D1%8F%D0%BC_%D0%BA%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%BC%D0%BE%D1%81%D0%B0%C2%BB_%2814006733426%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Monument_to_the_Conquerors_of_Space" title="Monument to the Conquerors of Space">Monument to the Conquerors of Space</a></div>
</div>
<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:197px;max-width:197px">
<div class="thumbimage"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="600" height="260" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg/195px-The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg/293px-The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg/390px-The_Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman.jpg 2x" width="195"/></a></div>
<div class="thumbcaption" style="clear:left"><a href="/wiki/Worker_and_Kolkhoz_Woman" title="Worker and Kolkhoz Woman">Worker and Kolkhoz Woman</a> sculpture in <a href="/wiki/VDNKh_(Russia)" title="VDNKh (Russia)">VDNKh</a></div>
</div>
<div style="clear:left"></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Plaques on house exteriors will inform passers-by that a well-known personality once lived there. Frequently, the plaques are dedicated to Soviet celebrities not well-known outside (or often, like with decorated generals and revolutionaries, now both inside) of Russia. There are also many "museum houses" of famous Russian writers, composers, and artists in the city.</p>
<p>Moscow's skyline is quickly modernizing with several new towers under construction. In recent years, the city administration has been widely criticized for heavy destruction that has affected many historical buildings. As much as a third of historic Moscow has been destroyed in the past few years<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> to make space for luxury apartments and hotels.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Other historical buildings, including such landmarks as the 1930 Moskva hotel and the 1913 department store Voyentorg, have been razed and reconstructed anew, with the inevitable loss of historical value. Critics blame the government for not enforcing conservation laws: in the last 12 years more than 50 buildings with monument status were torn down, several of those dating back to the 17th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Some critics also wonder if the money used for the reconstruction of razed buildings could not be used for the renovation of decaying structures, which include many works by architect Konstantin Melnikov<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Mayakovskaya_(Moscow_Metro)" title="Mayakovskaya (Moscow Metro)">Mayakovskaya</a> metro station.</p>
<p>Some organizations, such as Moscow Architecture Preservation Society<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> and Save Europe's Heritage,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup> are trying to draw the international public attention to these problems.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parks_and_landmarks">Parks and landmarks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Parks and landmarks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Voitonpuisto_Moskovassa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Victory Park on <a href="/wiki/Poklonnaya_Hill" title="Poklonnaya Hill">Poklonnaya Hill</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are 96 parks and 18 gardens in Moscow, including four botanical gardens. There are 450 square kilometres (170 sq mi) of green zones besides 100 square kilometres (39 sq mi) of forests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mosru-stats_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-mosru-stats-79">[79]</a></sup> Moscow is a very green city, if compared to other cities of comparable size in Western Europe and North America; this is partly due to a history of having green "yards" with trees and grass, between residential buildings. There are on average 27 square meters (290 sq ft) of parks per person in Moscow compared with 6 for <a href="/wiki/Paris" title="Paris">Paris</a>, 7.5 in <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a> and 8.6 in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greendress_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-greendress-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gorky_Park_(Moscow)" title="Gorky Park (Moscow)">Gorky Park</a> (officially the <a href="/wiki/Gorky_Park_(Moscow)" title="Gorky Park (Moscow)">Central Park of Culture and Rest</a> named after <a href="/wiki/Maxim_Gorky" title="Maxim Gorky">Maxim Gorky</a>), was founded in 1928. The main part (689,000 square metres or 170 <a href="/wiki/Acre" title="Acre">acres</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greendress_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-greendress-80">[80]</a></sup> along the Moskva river contains <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Architectural_glossary#Estrade" title="Architectural glossary">estrades</a>, children's attractions (including the <i>Observation Wheel</i> water ponds with boats and water bicycles), dancing, tennis courts and other sports facilities. It borders the <a href="/wiki/Neskuchny_Garden" title="Neskuchny Garden">Neskuchny Garden</a> (408,000 square metres or 101 acres), the oldest park in Moscow and a former imperial residence, created as a result of the integration of three estates in the 18th century. The Garden features the Green Theater, one of the largest open amphitheaters in Europe, able to hold up to 15 thousand people.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<p>Several parks include a section known as a "Park of Culture and Rest", sometimes alongside a much wilder area (this includes parks such as Izmaylovsky, Fili and Sokolniki). Some parks are designated as Forest Parks (lesopark).</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Izmaylovsky_Park" title="Izmaylovsky Park">Izmaylovsky Park</a>, created in 1931, is one of the largest urban parks in the world along with <a href="/wiki/Richmond_Park" title="Richmond Park">Richmond Park</a> in London. Its area of 15.34 square kilometres (5.92 sq mi) is six times greater than that of <a href="/wiki/Central_Park" title="Central Park">Central Park</a> in New York.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greendress_80-2"><a href="#cite_note-greendress-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Sokolniki_Park" title="Sokolniki Park">Sokolniki Park</a>, named after the <a href="/wiki/Falcon" title="Falcon">falcon</a> hunting that occurred there in the past, is one of the oldest parks in Moscow and has an area of 6 square kilometres (2.3 sq mi). A central circle with a large fountain is surrounded by birch, maple and elm tree alleys. A labyrinth composed of green paths lies beyond the park's ponds.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Losiny_Ostrov_National_Park" title="Losiny Ostrov National Park">Losiny Ostrov National Park</a> ("Elk Island" National Park), with a total area of more than 116 square kilometres (45 sq mi), borders Sokolniki Park and was Russia's first national park. It is quite wild, and is also known as the "city taiga" – elk can be seen there.</p>
<p>Tsytsin Main <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Botanical_Garden_of_Academy_of_Sciences" title="Moscow Botanical Garden of Academy of Sciences">Botanical Garden of Academy of Sciences</a>, founded in 1945 is the largest in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> It covers territory of 3.61 square kilometres (1.39 sq mi) bordering the All-Russia Exhibition Center and contains a live exhibition of more than 20 thousand species of plants from around the world, as well as a lab for scientific research. It contains a rosarium with 20 thousand rose bushes, a dendrarium, and an oak forest, with the average age of trees exceeding 100 years. There is a greenhouse taking up more than 5,000 square metres (53,820 square feet) of land.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-greendress_80-3"><a href="#cite_note-greendress-80">[80]</a></sup></p>
<p>The All-Russian Exhibition Center (Всероссийский выставочный центр), formerly known as the All-Union Agricultural Exhibition (VSKhV) and later Exhibition of Achievements of the National Economy (VDNKh), though officially named a "permanent trade show", is one of the most prominent examples of Stalinist-era monumental architecture. Among the large spans of recreational park areas are scores of elaborate pavilions, each representing either a branch of Soviet industry and science or a USSR republic. Even though during the 1990s it was, and for some part still is, misused as a gigantic shopping center (most of the pavilions are rented out for small businesses), it still retains the bulk of its architectural landmarks, including two monumental fountains (<i><a href="/wiki/The_Stone_Flower_Fountain" title="The Stone Flower Fountain">Stone Flower</a></i> and <i>Friendship of Nations</i>) and a 360 degrees panoramic cinema. In 2014 the park returned to the name Exhibition of Achievements of National Economy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>Lilac Park</i>, founded in 1958, has a permanent sculpture display and a large rosarium.</p>
<p>Moscow has always been a popular destination for tourists. Some of the more famous attractions include the city's UNESCO World Heritage Site, Moscow Kremlin and Red Square,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> which was built between the 14th and 17th centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Kolomenskoye#The_White_Column_of_Kolomenskoye" title="Kolomenskoye">Church of the Ascension</a> at Kolomenskoye, which dates from 1532, is also a UNESCO World Heritage Site and another popular attraction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>
<p>Near the <a href="/wiki/Tretyakov_Gallery" title="Tretyakov Gallery">new Tretyakov Gallery</a> there is a sculpture garden, Museon, often called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_graveyard_of_fallen_monuments" title="The graveyard of fallen monuments">the graveyard of fallen monuments</a>" that displays statues of the former Soviet Union that were removed from their place after its dissolution.</p>
<p>Other attractions include the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Zoo" title="Moscow Zoo">Moscow Zoo</a>, a zoological garden in two sections (the valleys of two streams) linked by a bridge, with nearly a thousand species and more than 6,500 specimens.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moszoo_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-moszoo-87">[87]</a></sup> Each year, the zoo attracts more than 1.2 million visitors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-moszoo_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-moszoo-87">[87]</a></sup> Many of Moscow's parks and landscaped gardens are protected natural environments.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Moscow_rings">Moscow rings</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Moscow rings">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ISS-30_Moscow,_Russia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2832" data-file-width="4256" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg/220px-ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg/330px-ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg/440px-ISS-30_Moscow%2C_Russia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ISS-30_Moscow,_Russia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The concentric and outward spanning roads of Moscow, as viewed from the <a href="/wiki/International_Space_Station" title="International Space Station">International Space Station</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="825" data-file-width="1100" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg/200px-Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg/300px-Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b4/Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg/400px-Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Third_Ring_near_Moscow_City.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Third_Ring_Road_(Moscow)" title="Third Ring Road (Moscow)">Third Ring Road</a> near <a href="/wiki/Moscow_International_Business_Center" title="Moscow International Business Center">Moscow-City</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moscow's road system is centered roughly on the Kremlin at the heart of the city. From there, roads generally span outwards to intersect with a sequence of circular roads ("rings").</p>
<p>The first and innermost major ring, Bulvarnoye Koltso (<a href="/wiki/Boulevard_Ring" title="Boulevard Ring">Boulevard Ring</a>), was built at the former location of the 16th-century city wall around what used to be called <a href="/wiki/Bely_Gorod" title="Bely Gorod">Bely Gorod</a> (White Town).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golden-ring_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-golden-ring-88">[88]</a></sup> The Bulvarnoye Koltso is technically not a ring; it does not form a complete circle, but instead a <a href="/wiki/Horseshoe" title="Horseshoe">horseshoe</a>-like arc that begins at the Cathedral of Christ the Savior and ends at the <a href="/wiki/Yauza_River" title="Yauza River">Yauza River</a>.</p>
<p>The second primary ring, located outside the bell end of the Boulevard Ring, is the Sadovoye Koltso (<a href="/wiki/Garden_Ring" title="Garden Ring">Garden Ring</a>). Like the Boulevard Ring, the Garden Ring follows the path of a 16th-century wall that used to encompass part of Moscow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-golden-ring_88-1"><a href="#cite_note-golden-ring-88">[88]</a></sup></p>
<p>The third ring, the Third Transport Ring, was completed in 2003 as a high-speed <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freeway" title="Freeway">freeway</a>.</p>
<p>The Fourth Transport Ring, another freeway, is under construction to further reduce traffic congestion.</p>
<p>The outermost ring within Moscow is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MKAD" title="MKAD">Moscow Automobile Ring Road</a> (often called the <i>MKAD</i> from the Russian <i>Московская Кольцевая Автомобильная Дорога</i>), which forms the approximate boundary of the city, was established in the 1950s. Outside Moscow, some of the roads encompassing the city continue to follow this circular pattern seen inside city limits.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Life_and_culture">Life and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Life and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Overview">Overview</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Overview">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">

<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Moscow_International_House_of_Music.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Moscow_International_House_of_Music" title="Moscow International House of Music">Moscow International House of Music</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>One of the most notable art museums in Moscow is the <a href="/wiki/Tretyakov_Gallery" title="Tretyakov Gallery">Tretyakov Gallery</a>, which was founded by <a href="/wiki/Pavel_Tretyakov" title="Pavel Tretyakov">Pavel Tretyakov</a>, a wealthy patron of the arts who donated a large private collection to the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> The Tretyakov Gallery is split into two buildings. The Old Tretyakov gallery, the original gallery in the <a href="/wiki/Tretyakov_Gallery" title="Tretyakov Gallery">Tretyakovskaya</a> area on the south bank of the Moskva River, houses works in the classic Russian tradition.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trety_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-trety-90">[90]</a></sup> The works of famous pre-<a href="/wiki/October_Revolution" title="October Revolution">Revolutionary</a> painters, such as <a href="/wiki/Ilya_Repin" title="Ilya Repin">Ilya Repin</a>, as well as the works of early Russian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Icon_painting" title="Icon painting">icon painters</a> can be found here. Visitors can even see rare originals by early 15th-century <a href="/wiki/Iconography" title="Iconography">iconographer</a> <a href="/wiki/Andrei_Rublev" title="Andrei Rublev">Andrei Rublev</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-trety_90-1"><a href="#cite_note-trety-90">[90]</a></sup> The New Tretyakov gallery, created in Soviet times, mainly contains the works of Soviet artists, as well as of a few contemporary paintings, but there is some overlap with the Old Tretyakov Gallery for early 20th-century art. The new gallery includes a small reconstruction of <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Tatlin" title="Vladimir Tatlin">Vladimir Tatlin</a>'s famous <i><a href="/wiki/Tatlin%27s_Tower" title="Tatlin's Tower">Monument to the Third International</a></i> and a mixture of other avant-garde works by artists like <a href="/wiki/Kazimir_Malevich" title="Kazimir Malevich">Kazimir Malevich</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wassily_Kandinsky" title="Wassily Kandinsky">Wassily Kandinsky</a>. Socialist realism features can also be found within the halls of the New Tretyakov Gallery.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gmii.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="480" data-file-width="640" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Gmii.jpg/200px-Gmii.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Gmii.jpg/300px-Gmii.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Gmii.jpg/400px-Gmii.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gmii.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The <a href="/wiki/Pushkin_Museum" title="Pushkin Museum">Pushkin Museum of Fine Arts</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Another art museum in the city of Moscow is the <a href="/wiki/Pushkin_Museum" title="Pushkin Museum">Pushkin Museum of Fine Arts</a>, which was founded by, among others, the father of <a href="/wiki/Marina_Tsvetaeva" title="Marina Tsvetaeva">Marina Tsvetaeva</a>. The Pushkin Museum is similar to the <a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a> in London in that its halls are a cross-section of exhibits on world civilisations, with many copies of ancient sculptures. However, it also hosts paintings from every major Western era; works by <a href="/wiki/Claude_Monet" title="Claude Monet">Claude Monet</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paul_C%C3%A9zanne" title="Paul Cézanne">Paul Cézanne</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pablo_Picasso" title="Pablo Picasso">Pablo Picasso</a> are present in the museum's collection.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/State_Historical_Museum" title="State Historical Museum">State Historical Museum</a> of Russia (Государственный Исторический музей) is a museum of Russian history located between Red Square and <a href="/wiki/Manezhnaya_Square,_Moscow" title="Manezhnaya Square, Moscow">Manege Square</a> in Moscow. Its exhibitions range from relics of the prehistoric tribes inhabiting present-day Russia, through priceless artworks acquired by members of the Romanov dynasty. The total number of objects in the museum's collection numbers is several million. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytechnical_Museum" title="Polytechnical Museum">Polytechnical Museum</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> founded in 1872 is the largest technical museum in Russia, offering a wide array of historical inventions and technological achievements, including humanoid automata from the 18th century and the first Soviet computers. Its collection contains more than 160,000 items.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The Borodino Panorama<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> museum located on Kutuzov Avenue provides an opportunity for visitors to experience being on a battlefield with a 360° <a href="/wiki/Diorama" title="Diorama">diorama</a>. It is a part of the large historical memorial commemorating the victory in the Patriotic War of 1812 over Napoleon’s army, that includes also the <a href="/wiki/Triumphal_Arch_of_Moscow" title="Triumphal Arch of Moscow">triumphal arch</a>, erected in 1827. There is also a military history museum that includes statues, and military hardware.</p>
<p>Moscow is the heart of the Russian performing arts, including <a href="/wiki/Russian_ballet" title="Russian ballet">ballet</a> and film, with 68 museums<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> 103<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> theaters, 132 cinemas and 24 concert halls. Among Moscow’s theaters and ballet studios is the <a href="/wiki/Bolshoi_Theatre" title="Bolshoi Theatre">Bolshoi Theatre</a> and the Malyi Theatre as well as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vakhtangov_Theatre" title="Vakhtangov Theatre">Vakhtangov Theatre</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Art_Theatre" title="Moscow Art Theatre">Moscow Art Theatre</a>.</p>
<p>The Moscow International Performance Arts Center,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> opened in 2003, also known as <a href="/wiki/Moscow_International_House_of_Music" title="Moscow International House of Music">Moscow International House of Music</a>, is known for its performances in classical music. It has the largest <a href="/wiki/Organ_(music)" title="Organ (music)">organ</a> in Russia installed in Svetlanov Hall.</p>
<p>There are also two large circuses in Moscow: <a href="/wiki/Moscow_State_Circus" title="Moscow State Circus">Moscow State Circus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Circus_on_Tsvetnoy_Boulevard" title="Moscow Circus on Tsvetnoy Boulevard">Moscow Circus on Tsvetnoy Boulevard</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> named after <a href="/wiki/Yuri_Nikulin" title="Yuri Nikulin">Yuri Nikulin</a>.</p>
<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Memorial_Museum_of_Astronautics" title="Memorial Museum of Astronautics">Memorial Museum of Astronautics</a> under the <a href="/wiki/Monument_to_the_Conquerors_of_Space" title="Monument to the Conquerors of Space">Monument to the Conquerors of Space</a> in the end of <a href="/wiki/Cosmonauts_Alley" title="Cosmonauts Alley">Cosmonauts Alley</a> is the central memorial place for the Russian space officials.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Mosfilm" title="Mosfilm">Mosfilm</a> studio was at the heart of many classic films, as it is responsible for both artistic and mainstream productions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup> However, despite the continued presence and reputation of internationally renowned Russian filmmakers, the once prolific native studios are much quieter. Rare and historical films may be seen in the Salut cinema, where films from the Museum of Cinema<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> collection are shown regularly.</p>
<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shchusev_State_Museum_of_Architecture" title="Shchusev State Museum of Architecture">Shchusev State Museum of Architecture</a> is the national museum of Russian architecture by the name of the architect <a href="/wiki/Alexey_Shchusev" title="Alexey Shchusev">Alexey Shchusev</a> near the Kremlin area.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Sports">Sports</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Sports">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Over 500 Olympic sports champions lived in the city by 2005.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> Moscow is home to 63 stadiums (besides eight football and eleven light athletics maneges), of which Luzhniki Stadium is the largest and the 4th biggest in Europe (it hosted the <a href="/wiki/1998%E2%80%9399_UEFA_Cup" title="1998–99 UEFA Cup">1998–99 UEFA Cup</a>, <a href="/wiki/2007%E2%80%9308_UEFA_Champions_League" title="2007–08 UEFA Champions League">2007–08 UEFA Champions League</a> finals, and the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a>, and will host the <a href="/wiki/2018_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2018 FIFA World Cup">2018 FIFA World Cup</a> with 6 games total). Forty other sport complexes are located within the city, including 24 with artificial ice. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Olympic_Stadium_(Moscow_arena)" title="Olympic Stadium (Moscow arena)">Olympic Stadium</a> was the world's first indoor arena for <a href="/wiki/Bandy" title="Bandy">bandy</a> and hosted the <a href="/wiki/Bandy_World_Championship" title="Bandy World Championship">Bandy World Championship</a> twice.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup> Moscow was again the host of the competition in 2010, this time in Krylatskoye.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> That arena has also hosted the <a href="/wiki/World_Speed_Skating_Championships" title="World Speed Skating Championships">World Speed Skating Championships</a>. There are also seven <a href="/wiki/Horse_racing" title="Horse racing">horse racing</a> tracks in Moscow,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mosru-stats_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-mosru-stats-79">[79]</a></sup> of which <a href="/wiki/Central_Moscow_Hippodrome" title="Central Moscow Hippodrome">Central Moscow Hippodrome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> founded in 1834, is the largest.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2048" data-file-width="3820" height="118" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg/220px-Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg/330px-Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg/440px-Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Flickr_-_Pavel_Kazachkov_-_Luzhniki_Olympic_Complex._Grand_Sports_Arena..jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Luzhniki_Stadium" title="Luzhniki Stadium">Luzhniki Stadium</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moscow was the host city of the <a href="/wiki/1980_Summer_Olympics" title="1980 Summer Olympics">1980 Summer Olympics</a>, with the <a href="/wiki/Yachting" title="Yachting">yachting</a> events being held at <a href="/wiki/Tallinn" title="Tallinn">Tallinn</a>, in present-day <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a>. Large sports facilities and the main international airport, Sheremetyevo Terminal 2, were built in preparation for the 1980 Summer Olympics. Moscow had made a bid for the <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">2012 Summer Olympics</a>. However, when final voting commenced on July 6, 2005, Moscow was the first city to be eliminated from further rounds. The Games were awarded to <a href="/wiki/2012_Summer_Olympics" title="2012 Summer Olympics">London</a>.</p>
<p>The most titled <a href="/wiki/Ice_hockey" title="Ice hockey">ice hockey</a> team in the Soviet Union and in the world, <a href="/wiki/HC_CSKA_Moscow" title="HC CSKA Moscow">HC CSKA Moscow</a> comes from Moscow. Other big ice hockey clubs from Moscow are <a href="/wiki/HC_Dynamo_Moscow" title="HC Dynamo Moscow">HC Dynamo Moscow</a>, which was the second most titled team in the Soviet Union, and <a href="/wiki/HC_Spartak_Moscow" title="HC Spartak Moscow">HC Spartak Moscow</a>.</p>
<p>The most titled Soviet, Russian, and one of the most titled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euroleague" title="Euroleague">Euroleague</a> clubs, is the <a href="/wiki/Basketball" title="Basketball">basketball</a> club from Moscow <a href="/wiki/PBC_CSKA_Moscow" title="PBC CSKA Moscow">PBC CSKA Moscow</a>. Another strong basketball club from Moscow is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MBC_Dynamo_Moscow" title="MBC Dynamo Moscow">MBC Dynamo Moscow</a>. Moscow hosted the <a href="/wiki/EuroBasket" title="EuroBasket">EuroBasket</a> in 1953 and 1965.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1063" data-file-width="1600" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg/220px-Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg/330px-Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/50/Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg/440px-Stadium_Spartak_in_Moscow.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arena_CSKA.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="394" data-file-width="700" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Arena_CSKA.jpg/220px-Arena_CSKA.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Arena_CSKA.jpg/330px-Arena_CSKA.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Arena_CSKA.jpg/440px-Arena_CSKA.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>

<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CSKA_Moscow_Stadium" title="CSKA Moscow Stadium">Arena CSKA</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Moscow had more winners at the <a href="/wiki/USSR_Chess_Championship" title="USSR Chess Championship">USSR</a> and <a href="/wiki/Russian_Chess_Championship" title="Russian Chess Championship">Russian Chess Championship</a> than any other city.</p>
<p>The most titled <a href="/wiki/Volleyball" title="Volleyball">volleyball</a> team in the Soviet Union and in Europe (<a href="/wiki/CEV_Champions_League" title="CEV Champions League">CEV Champions League</a>) is <a href="/wiki/VC_CSKA_Moscow" title="VC CSKA Moscow">VC CSKA Moscow</a>.</p>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a>, <a href="/wiki/FC_Spartak_Moscow" title="FC Spartak Moscow">FC Spartak Moscow</a> has won more championship titles in the <a href="/wiki/Russian_Premier_League" title="Russian Premier League">Russian Premier League</a> than any other team. They were second only to <a href="/wiki/FC_Dynamo_Kyiv" title="FC Dynamo Kyiv">FC Dynamo Kyiv</a> in the Soviet Union. <a href="/wiki/PFC_CSKA_Moscow" title="PFC CSKA Moscow">PFC CSKA Moscow</a> was the first Russian football team to win a <a href="/wiki/UEFA" title="UEFA">UEFA</a> title. <a href="/wiki/FC_Lokomotiv_Moscow" title="FC Lokomotiv Moscow">FC Lokomotiv Moscow</a>, <a href="/wiki/FC_Dynamo_Moscow" title="FC Dynamo Moscow">FC Dynamo Moscow</a> and <a href="/wiki/FC_Torpedo_Moscow" title="FC Torpedo Moscow">FC Torpedo Moscow</a> are other professional football teams also based in Moscow.</p>
<p>Moscow houses other prominent football, ice hockey, and basketball teams. Because sports organisations in the Soviet Union were once highly centralized, two of the best Union-level teams represented defence and law-enforcing agencies: the Armed Forces (CSKA) and the Ministry of Internal Affairs (Dinamo). There were army and police teams in most major cities. A a result Spartak, CSKA, and Dinamo were among the best-funded teams in the USSR.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="501" data-file-width="1000" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg/220px-Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg/330px-Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg/440px-Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lokomotiv_Stadium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Lokomotiv_Stadium_(Moscow)" title="Lokomotiv Stadium (Moscow)">Lokomotiv Stadium</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Because of Moscow's cold local climate, <a href="/wiki/Winter_sport" title="Winter sport">winter sports</a> have a following. Many of Moscow's large parks offer marked trails for skiing and frozen ponds for skating.</p>
<p>Moscow hosts the annual <a href="/wiki/Kremlin_Cup" title="Kremlin Cup">Kremlin Cup</a>, a popular tennis tournament on both the <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_Tennis_Association" title="Women's Tennis Association">WTA</a> and <a href="/wiki/Association_of_Tennis_Professionals" title="Association of Tennis Professionals">ATP</a> tours. It is one of the ten Tier-I events on the women's tour and a host of Russian players feature every year.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="700" data-file-width="1050" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg/220px-VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg/330px-VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/08/VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg/440px-VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:VTB_Ice_Palace1.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/VTB_Ice_Palace" title="VTB Ice Palace">VTB Ice Palace</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Slava_Moscow" title="Slava Moscow">Slava Moscow</a> is a professional <a href="/wiki/Rugby_union" title="Rugby union">rugby</a> club, competing in the national <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Professional_Rugby_League" title="Professional Rugby League">Professional Rugby League</a>. Former <a href="/wiki/Rugby_league" title="Rugby league">rugby league</a> heavyweights <a href="/wiki/RC_Lokomotiv_Moscow" title="RC Lokomotiv Moscow">RC Lokomotiv</a> have entered the same league as of 2011<sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>. The Luzhniki Stadium also hosted the <a href="/wiki/2013_Rugby_World_Cup_Sevens" title="2013 Rugby World Cup Sevens">2013 Rugby World Cup Sevens</a>.</p>
<p>In the game of bandy, one of the most successful clubs in the world is 20 times and current <a href="/wiki/Russian_Bandy_Super_League" title="Russian Bandy Super League">Russian League</a> champions Dynamo Moscow. They have also won the <a href="/wiki/Bandy_World_Cup" title="Bandy World Cup">World Cup</a> twice and <a href="/wiki/European_Cup_(bandy)" title="European Cup (bandy)">European Cup</a> six times.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/MFK_Dinamo_Moskva" title="MFK Dinamo Moskva">MFK Dinamo Moskva</a> is one of the major <a href="/wiki/Futsal" title="Futsal">futsal</a> clubs in Europe.</p>
<p>Russia was given the right to host the <a href="/wiki/2018_FIFA_World_Cup" title="2018 FIFA World Cup">2018 FIFA World Cup</a>, and in Moscow, the Luzhniki Stadium will have an increased capacity, by almost 10,000 new seats, in addition to a further two stadiums that will be built: the Dynamo Stadium, and the Spartak Stadium. Together these will have a capacity of at least 40,000 seats.</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Entertainment">Entertainment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Moscow&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Entertainment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="487" data-file-width="1024" height="105" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png/220px-Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png/330px-Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6c/Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png/440px-Fountain_moscow_europe_square_night_bird.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
